                        <div class="col-md-12">
                            <div class="section-title page-title">
								<h1 class="main-title">ICS 163 &#8211; Mobile &#038; Ubiquitous Games</h1>                                <div class="bread-crumb" typeof="BreadcrumbList" vocab="http://schema.org/">
									                                </div>

    <div class="content-inner">
        <div class="container">

                    <div class="page-content-area">
                        <article id="post-651" class="post-651 page type-page status-publish hentry">
                            <div class="entry-content">
                                		<div data-elementor-type="post" data-elementor-id="651" class="elementor elementor-651" data-elementor-settings="[]">
			<div class="elementor-inner">
				<div class="elementor-section-wrap">
							<section class="elementor-element elementor-element-5505dcab elementor-section-boxed elementor-section-height-default elementor-section-height-default elementor-section elementor-top-section" data-id="5505dcab" data-element_type="section" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
						<div class="elementor-container elementor-column-gap-default">
				<div class="elementor-row">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-9753a85 elementor-column elementor-col-100 elementor-top-column" data-id="9753a85" data-element_type="column">
			<div class="elementor-column-wrap  elementor-element-populated">
					<div class="elementor-widget-wrap">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-a616155 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="a616155" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<div class="elementor-text-editor elementor-clearfix"><p>Computer Game Science Major<br />Spring 2016</p><p>Professor Theresa Jean Tanenbaum,<br />Email: <a href="mailto:joshua.tanenbaum@uci.edu">t</a>tanen@uci.edu<br />Teaching Assistant: Daniel Gardner<br />Email: <a href="mailto:DLGARDNE@UCI.EDU">dlgardne@uci.edu</a><br />Tu, Thur, 12:30-1:50pm<br />Location: SSL 270<br />Office Hours: By appointment<br />Course Website: <a href="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/classes/ics-163-mobile-ubiquitous-games/">https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/classes/ics-163-mobile-ubiquitous-games/</a></p><h1>Catalog Description:</h1><p>Design and technology of mobile games, including mixed reality gaming, urban games, and locative media. Case studies of significant systems. Uses and limitations of location-based technologies. Infrastructures and their relationships to gameplay and design.</p><h1>Course Objectives and Philosophy</h1><p>This class will combine game playing, game design, and location-based technology to introduce you to the challenges and opportunities that currently exist within the mobile and ubiquitous games design space.  We will be playing many classic locative games – both digital and analog – and will be designing, implementing and testing our own new location based games.  By the end of this class, you should be able to:</p><ul><li>Discuss the history of locative gaming and understand how to situate a contemporary game within this context by identifying key influences on the current state of mobile games.</li><li>Understand and be able to apply key mobile game design concepts (such as time, synchronicity, location, public/private play, space and place, movement and mobility) and be able to identify key differences between the design of pervasive vs non-pervasive games.</li><li>Develop a familiarity with the core technologies that support the development of pervasive games including their advantages and limitations, the major challenges facing developers of pervasive games, and the possible futures for these technologies.</li><li>In a small group (4-6 people) you will design, test, and deploy a pervasive game that takes advantage of the <em>specific unique affordances of the UCI campus</em>.</li><li>Document and describe your locative or spatial game in order to communicate and preserve the experience for people who are unable to play it for themselves.</li></ul><p>This class is <strong><em>not</em> </strong>going to be about creating games for mobile phones or tablets. Games made in this class may well involve Android or iOS development, but if you are interested in making Flappy Bird, Angry Birds, or any other bird related game to be played on a mobile device, you are in the wrong place.</p><p>Some of you are programmers.  Others are not.  I am not a programmer, and so I will not be evaluating you based on your code.  I will be evaluating you based on your understanding of the concepts, the sophistication with which you integrate them into your designs, and the overall experience that you create.</p><h1>Course Logistics</h1><h2>Course Website</h2><p>I will post the syllabus and assignments on the website, along with link to reading quizzes and other online resources.  If we make any changes to the class structure or deliverables, these will be reflected on the website.  If there is any disagreement between this printed syllabus and the website, the website should be considered the authoritative and up-to-date reference.</p><h2>Equipment</h2><p>Every student will need access to a computer and to a GPS enabled device (ideally a smartphone that is less than 4 years old).</p><h2>Internet Access</h2><p>All students should be able access the internet in order to complete quizzes and submit assignments.</p><h1>Assignments and Grading and Schedule</h1><h2>Course Structure</h2><p>We meet twice a week.  The first class of the week is usually some combination of discussion and lecture, while the second class of the week is usually some sort of group activity or time to work on projects and receive feedback.</p><h2>Index Cards and Participation</h2><p>Class attendance will be determined by completing index cards. The index cards are also a means for me to get feedback about the course.</p><p>For each class please write your name on a card, the date, your student ID and a comment about the course.</p><p>If you would like to submit an anonymous comment, take an extra card and don&#8217;t put your name on it.</p><p>Collecting feedback this way is a useful and different source of input than other media. It helps me track how the class is understanding the material and something about writing things out on paper causes students to say different things than they would in an online system.</p><h2>Readings and Quizzes:</h2><p>The main textbook for this course will be <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Pervasive-Games-Theory-Design-Kaufmann/dp/0123748534/ref=sr_1_1?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1457470487&amp;sr=8-1&amp;keywords=waern+pervasive+games"><strong><em>Pervasive Games: Experiences on the Boundary Between Life and Play</em></strong></a> by Markus Montola, Jakko Sternos, and Annika Waern.  Additional “readings” will be assigned periodically, and may include texts, videos, or games.  Most readings will have a quiz associated with them in EEE. There will be 10 quizzes on the readings, each of which will be worth 2% of your grade, for a total of 20% of your overall grade.</p><h2><a href="https://drive.google.com/open?id=0ByXU8gAAONgoVGduTkQtai1YY0E">Assignment #1: Historical Analysis</a></h2><p>Assignment 1 is a short individual writing assignment where you will analyze a contemporary game within its historical context (details will be circulated when it is assigned).</p><h2><a href="https://drive.google.com/open?id=0ByXU8gAAONgoU1BPc2F5eGRMS0E">Assignment #2: Geocaching Journal</a></h2><p>Assignment 2 is a group project that will require you to discover and design local geocaches (details will be circulated when it is assigned).</p><h2><a href="https://drive.google.com/open?id=0ByXU8gAAONgoR3BsY0wzN3FuMGs">Final Project: Design, Implement, and Document a Pervasive Game</a></h2><p>For your final project you will design, implement, and document a pervasive game.  This game may be digital or analog, or a combination of both, but it must incorporate some specific aspect of the UCI campus and its surroundings into the gameplay in a meaningful way (details will be circulated when it is assigned).</p><h2>Grade Breakdown and Rubric</h2><ul><li>Index Cards: 10 points (.5 x 20) – Look! Just showing up for class is the difference between an entire letter grade!</li><li>Quizzes: 20 points (10 x 2 points each)</li><li>Historical Analysis: 15 Points (+ 1 possible point of extra credit)</li><li>Geocaching Journal: 15 points (+ 5 possible points of extra credit)</li><li>Final Project: 40 points (see breakdown below)<ul><li>Design Document: 5 Points</li><li>Preliminary Documentation: 5 points</li><li>Final Project Implementation: 15 Points*</li><li>Final Project Documentation: 15 Points*</li></ul></li></ul><p>Total Points available: 100 (+ up to 11 points of extra credit)</p><p>* Due to the situated nature of the final projects, in many cases the only way I will know what you did for your final project is through the documentation you submit.  Therefore it is in your best interest to submit documentation that is as comprehensive as possible, as much of your final grade will be determined by this.</p><h3>Extra Credit:</h3><p>There are several opportunities for extra credit in the class.</p><h4>Master Assassins</h4><p>We will be playing a game of Assassin that could conceivably last the entire quarter (if you are clever).  At the end of the quarter, your ranking in the assassin game will be worth a few extra credit points, as follows:</p><ul><li>Master Assassin (1): 5 points</li><li>Expert Assassins (2-10): 3 points</li><li>Honorable Assassins (11-30): 2 points</li><li>Assassins in Training (31 – 70): 1 point</li></ul><p>Dishonorable Assassins (students who cheat, or do not participate) will receive no extra credit points.</p><p>In the unlikely case that there is no master assassin victorious by the end of the quarter, a game of Ninja Slap will be used to determine the final rankings of any surviving assassins.</p><p><strong><a href="https://docs.google.com/spreadsheets/d/1zrs66OR_wds4ClrJ2w8LDuQt-6hoAgSaJgwICUoiaHo/edit#gid=0">Assassin Game Rankings!</a></strong></p><h4>Masters of the Future</h4><p>In assignment #1 (Historical Analysis) you may earn a single point of extra credit for proposing a convincing future scenario for a locative or pervasive game that evolves from the historical trajectory outlined in the rest of your submission.  You should not use more than 200 extra words to complete this extra credit.</p><h4>Geocaching Masters</h4><p>To earn the title of Geocaching Master, you will need to submit your new cache to the geocaching.com website (see the full assignment for details).  If it gets approved before the end of the quarter, I will award 5 points of extra credit to your entire team. This offer is only good once – additional approved caches will not yield additional points. This will certainly be a difficult the approval process, and I’m not certain how likely it is that your cache will be accepted so this might be an easy 2 points, or it might be virtually impossible to earn.  That’s why it’s <em>extra</em>.</p><h2>Assignment Submission</h2><p>Individual written assignments will be submitted via turnitin.com.  Group assignments will be uploaded to the class Google Drive folder.  Quizzes will be completed in EEE, and will take place in class.</p><h1>Schedule</h1><p>This is a day-by-day breakdown of the class structure.  Some of this is likely to change, in which case refer to the website for the up-to-date version of the schedule.</p><p>Assassin: Rules &amp; The UCI campus policy on academic honesty resides here: <a href="http://honesty.uci.edu/">http://honesty.uci.edu/</a></p><p>The penalty for plagiarism is at a minimum to receive a 0 on the assignment and have the case reported to the Associate Dean’s office. Particularly flagrant cases may receive more severe punishment (notably failing the course).</p><p><em><strong>What is cheating?</strong></em></p><ul><li><em>Supplying or using work or answers that are not your own.</em></li><li><em>Providing or accepting assistance with completing assignments or examinations.</em></li><li><em>Faking data or results.</em></li><li><em>Interfering in any way with someone else’s work.</em></li><li><em>Stealing an examination or solution from the teacher.</em></li></ul><p><em><strong>What is plagiarism?</strong></em></p><ul><li><em>Copying a paper from a source text without proper acknowledgment.</em></li><li><em>Buying a paper from a research service or term paper mill.</em></li><li><em>Turning in another student’s work with or without that student’s knowledge. </em></li><li><em>Copying a paper from a source text without proper acknowledgment.</em></li><li><em>Copying materials from a source text, supplying proper documentation, but leaving out quotation marks.</em></li><li><em>Paraphrasing materials from a source text without appropriate documentation.</em></li><li><em>Turning in a paper from a term paper website.</em></li></ul><p>You should be on guard against plagiarism at all times.  At any time that you read anything in preparation for a paper or consciously recall anything that you have read or heard, you must be prepared to provide documentation.</p><p>Generally, when you use someone else’s ideas and/or words, you will either quote that person directly or you will paraphrase or summarize that person’s words. You must let the reader know which you are doing.</p><ol><li>If you quote the source directly, you must<ol><li>Put quotation marks before and after that person’s words</li><li>Let the reader know the source by (1) putting a footnote or endnote number at the end of the quotation, or (2) putting at least the source’s name in parentheses after the quotation marks (such as when being taken from fieldwork).</li></ol></li><li>If you paraphrase (a paraphrase is about the same length as the original, but in different words) or if you summarize (a summary is a severely shortened version of the original), you must<ol><li>Introduce the source in some manner at the beginning of the passage being paraphrased (or summarized) so that the reader can tell where your idea stops and the other person’s begins</li><li>State the ideas taken from the source in your own words and your own arrangement. It is possible to plagiarize sentence patterns as well as exact words. A handy rule: if, in a paraphrase or summary, you use a stretch of more than three words in their exact order from a source, you should put those words into quotation marks</li><li>Provide an exact source citation for those ideas paraphrased or summarized. This may be done either by footnote/endnote number at the end of the passages or by parenthetical references to the work and page(s). This citation provides credit to the author being used and allows the reader access to the material for further study.</li></ol></li><li>You must also provide a footnote for any chart, graph, figure, table, summary, or other data taken directly from another source or any information derived from such materials. You should also be sure to check copyright as to whether you are allowed to use this figure.</li></ol><p>For example, the text here on plagiarism was initially written Gillian Hayes for the Winter 2013 version of INF 242, although some modifications and additions of my own have been integrated into it.  The original can be found here:<em> </em><a href="http://www.gillianhayes.com/Inf242w13/"><em>http://www.gillianhayes.com/Inf242w13/</em></a>, along with Professor Hayes’ own disclaimer that the material has been “generously borrowed and slightly modified from the UTC Center for Advisement and Student Success.”</p><h2>Reference Management Software:</h2><p>Oftentimes plagiarism isn’t intentional – it happens because the writer either isn’t in the habit of citation, or because the overhead of citing sources turns the process into a burden. For this class I strongly encourage you to adopt the use of a reference management system if you do not already use one.  This is one of the single best investments of your time you can undertake as a student, and it will reward you a thousand times over once you have integrated it into your workflow.  Unless you are already heavily invested in a different platform, I would recommend you use <a href="https://www.zotero.org/"><strong>Zotero</strong></a><strong>:</strong> it’s free, it works as both a stand-alone program, and as a browser plug-in, it integrates very smoothly with Word, it has great collaboration support, it has AMAZING citation scrapers for the major online repositories (ACM DL, JSTOR, Springer, etc.), and it has a very complete database of reference formats that are easy to install.  Did I mention that it’s FREE?  It is! For more information about the options out there, the UCI Library has a good resource here:</p><p><a href="http://libguides.lib.uci.edu/content.php?pid=19606&amp;sid=583269">http://libguides.lib.uci.edu/content.php?pid=19606&amp;sid=583269</a>.</p><h2>Special Accommodations</h2><p>If you are a student with a disability (<em>e.g.</em>, physical, learning, psychiatric, vision, hearing, etc.) and think that you might need special assistance or a special accommodation in this class or any other class, please check out the Disability Center <a href="http://www.disability.uci.edu/">online</a> or visit them in person at: 100 Disability Services Center, Building 313, Irvine, CA 92697-5130.   If you are having difficulty with the class for any of these reasons, please let me know so that I can work with you to meet your learning needs. If for any reason you are uncomfortable discussing the details surrounding a given situation you need not disclose anything, but at least let me know that something is going on so that arrangements can be made to adjust things for you <em>before</em> you fall too far behind.</p><p>I am always available to meet with students who are having trouble, and I am usually willing to make some reasonable accommodations if you have a legitimate issue, but I <strong>require</strong> that you check-in with me <strong>before</strong> a problem gets out of control so we can work something out. If you come to me during finals week to explain that you were in the hospital for 6 weeks and couldn’t complete your work, my first question will be: “why am I just now finding this out?”</p><h2>Counseling Center</h2><p>Likewise, if you find that personal problems, career indecision, study and time management difficulties, etc. are adversely impacting your successful progress at UCI, please check out the Counseling Center or Graduate Student Services. Graduate school can often have adverse effects on one&#8217;s physical and mental health, and it is better to seek help early than allow the trials of pursing and advanced degree to cause serious harm.</p></div>

                            <div class="section-title page-title">
								<h1 class="main-title">Board Games Reclaimed!</h1>                                <div class="bread-crumb" typeof="BreadcrumbList" vocab="http://schema.org/">
									                                </div>
                            </div>

    <div class="content-inner">
        <div class="container">
            <div class="row">
                <div class="col-md-12">
                    <div class="page-content-area">
                        <article id="post-726" class="post-726 page type-page status-publish has-post-thumbnail hentry">
                            <div class="entry-content">
                                		<div data-elementor-type="post" data-elementor-id="726" class="elementor elementor-726" data-elementor-settings="[]">
			<div class="elementor-inner">
				<div class="elementor-section-wrap">
							<section class="elementor-element elementor-element-20252f8 elementor-section-stretched elementor-section-boxed elementor-section-height-default elementor-section-height-default elementor-section elementor-top-section" data-id="20252f8" data-element_type="section" data-settings="{&quot;stretch_section&quot;:&quot;section-stretched&quot;,&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
						<div class="elementor-container elementor-column-gap-default">
				<div class="elementor-row">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-2bd877b3 elementor-column elementor-col-100 elementor-top-column" data-id="2bd877b3" data-element_type="column">
			<div class="elementor-column-wrap  elementor-element-populated">
					<div class="elementor-widget-wrap">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-57242b99 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="57242b99" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<div class="elementor-text-editor elementor-clearfix"><p><img class="size-large wp-image-727 aligncenter" src="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu//wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/BGR_3-1024x576.jpg" alt="bgr_3" width="600" height="338" srcset="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/BGR_3-1024x576.jpg 1024w, https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/BGR_3-300x169.jpg 300w, https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/BGR_3-768x432.jpg 768w" sizes="(max-width: 600px) 100vw, 600px" /></p><p><strong>Evoke Lab (Room 2100), Calit2 Building, UCI</strong></p><p><strong>1-5 pm, </strong><strong>February 27, 2017</strong></p><p>Game designer Heather Logas (<a href="http://www.spaceforplay.com">Space for Play</a>) is coming to UC-Irvine to run an innovative, hands-on workshop on board game (re)design with Tess &amp; Karen Tanenbaum of the <a href="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/">Transformative Play Lab</a> (Department of <a href="http://www.informatics.uci.edu/">Informatics</a>). Board Games Reclaimed! gives students the opportunity to explore the iterative design process in a creative and playful way. In this workshop, students take a simple, familiar game (Candyland) and re-imagine a new set of rules, dynamics, interactions and mechanisms for it.</p><p>Teams of 4-5 participants will be tasked with “reclaiming” Candyland, both physically and mechanically.  Teams must choose one of two design goals: 1) create a party game for college students or 2) create a family night board game that every member of the family will enjoy (including younger kids, older kids and adults).  Copies of Candyland will be provided, as well as basic art materials, game making supplies (e.g. dice) and blank rule sheets.  Teams may use any additional supplies (art materials, found objects, their own bodies, etc) they wish in order to remake their games.  Iterative playtesting will be encouraged to help guide the re-design of the game. The workshop will conclude with a group reflection on the process and what was learned.</p><p>This event is sponsored by <a href="http://illuminations.uci.edu/">Illuminations</a> and primarily aimed at UCI undergraduates.</p><p><strong><span style="color: #ff0000;">Edit: The event is full! Thank you for your interest!</span></strong></p><p>Contact Karen Tanenbaum: karen.tanenbaum@gmail.com with any questions or problems.</p><p><strong>Participants</strong></p><p>The workshop is intended for ~30 undergraduate students.  Participants need not have any prior experience in games or other types of design in order to participate, and all majors are welcome.</p><p><strong>Planned Schedule</strong></p><p>Workshop runs 1 pm – 5pm in the Evoke Lab (Room 2100) in the Calit2 Building on UC-Irvine.</p><ul><li>Introductory Remarks: Welcome, introductions and team creation (15 min)</li><li>First Playtest: Teams play the original Candyland, taking notes about what could be changed to better fit their design goals (15 min)</li><li>Game Making Round 1: Teams attempt to add to and modify the game to better meet their goals (90 minutes)</li><li>Break (15 min): Coffee &amp; snacks provided</li><li>Second Playtest: Teams have a chance to play other teams’ games and give feedback (30 min)</li><li>Game Making Round 2: Teams re-group to discuss what they learned during the play testing and work on adapting the game, incorporating feedback (60 min)</li><li>Closing Remarks: Wrap up &amp; reflection on the process (15 min)</li></ul><p><strong>About Heather </strong></p><p>Heather Logas has been designing games professionally since 2004, teaching game design since 2008 and teaching game making through workshop style events since 2010. Making games is a specialized form of personal expression that uses rules and systems as raw materials. Making board games can be done collaboratively or solo and is an excellent way to physically manifest systems for careful examination and to share with others, making the process perfect for ideation around difficult problems.  Game makers in our workshops have created games about everything from meditation to business strategy. Heather is the founder of <a href="http://www.spaceforplay.com">Space for Play</a>, a consultancy dedicated to assisting organizations gain clarity to their challenges though system analysis via board game making.She has an MFA in Digital Arts &amp; New Media from the University of California-Santa Cruz and was a game designer and lecturer at the Center for Games &amp; Playable Media at UCSC. You can find her on twitter as <a href="https://twitter.com/systemsleuth">@systemsleuth</a>.</p></div>
				</div>
				</div>
						</div>
			</div>
		</div>
						</div>
			</div>
		</section>
						</div>
			</div>
		</div>
		                            </div><!-- .entry-content -->


                                                    </article><!-- #post-## -->
                    </div>
                </div>
            </div>
        </div>
    </div>
</div>

				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<div class="elementor-text-editor elementor-clearfix"><p>Aaron Trammell is an assistant professor of Informatics at UC Irvine. He graduated from the Rutgers University School of Communication and Information in 2015 and spent a year at the Annenberg School of Communication at USC as a postdoctoral researcher. Aaron’s research is focused on revealing historical connections between games, play, and the United States military-industrial complex. He is interested in how political and social ideology is integrated in the practice of game design and how these perspectives are negotiated within the imaginations of players. He is the Editor-in-Chief of the journal <i>Analog Game Studies</i> and the Multimedia Editor of <i>Sounding Out!</i></p></div>

						<div class="elementor-container elementor-column-gap-default">
				<div class="elementor-row">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-4aa05f03 elementor-column elementor-col-100 elementor-top-column" data-id="4aa05f03" data-element_type="column">
			<div class="elementor-column-wrap  elementor-element-populated">
					<div class="elementor-widget-wrap">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-15e55d53 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="15e55d53" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<div class="elementor-text-editor elementor-clearfix"><p>Dr Michael A. Cowling is a technology communicator and evangelist with a keen interest in educational technology, computer science education and digital ubiquity and acceptance. He is currently a Senior Lecturer in Educational Technology at CQUniversity Australia, where he is the founder of The CREATE Lab, which focuses on collaborative research and education around technology and education (<a href="http://www.thecreatelab.org">www.thecreatelab.org</a>). In 2016, Dr Cowling was the recipient of an Australian Government Citation for Outstanding Contributions to Student Learning; and in 2018 was appointed an Advance Queensland Community Digital Champion. He is a current partner in an Australian Office for Learning &amp; Teaching Innovation &amp; Development grant, is an assessor for the Australian Awards for University Teaching, and a consulting expert in information technology for the Australian Tertiary Education &amp; Quality Standards Association (TEQSA). He is also a frequent radio and newspaper commentator on digital technology and technology in society, and has written opinion pieces for national publications including the Sydney Morning Herald, the Melbourne Age, the Brisbane Times &amp; The Conversation. As a core tenant of his work, Dr Cowling lives by the mantra “pedagogy before technology”, and believes that all technology needs to maintain the underlying purpose to enrich our lives.</p></div>

								<h1 class="main-title">AR/VR Theater Syllabus</h1>                                <div class="bread-crumb" typeof="BreadcrumbList" vocab="http://schema.org/">

                    <div class="page-content-area">
                        <article id="post-1008" class="post-1008 page type-page status-publish hentry">
                            <div class="entry-content">
                                		<div data-elementor-type="post" data-elementor-id="1008" class="elementor elementor-1008" data-elementor-settings="[]">
			<div class="elementor-inner">
				<div class="elementor-section-wrap">
							<section class="elementor-element elementor-element-183fd0ae elementor-section-boxed elementor-section-height-default elementor-section-height-default elementor-section elementor-top-section" data-id="183fd0ae" data-element_type="section" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
						<div class="elementor-container elementor-column-gap-default">
				<div class="elementor-row">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-fbbfd30 elementor-column elementor-col-100 elementor-top-column" data-id="fbbfd30" data-element_type="column">
			<div class="elementor-column-wrap  elementor-element-populated">
					<div class="elementor-widget-wrap">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-1c9b3e elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="1c9b3e" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<div class="elementor-text-editor elementor-clearfix"><p style="text-align: center;"><img class="size-medium wp-image-677 alignleft" src="http://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/TPL-Logo-BW-words-300x184.jpg" alt="" width="300" height="184" srcset="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/TPL-Logo-BW-words-300x184.jpg 300w, https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/TPL-Logo-BW-words.jpg 614w" sizes="(max-width: 300px) 100vw, 300px" /><img class="wp-image-1009 alignleft" src="http://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/ApplesOrangesrts_logo_round_blue-298x300.png" alt="" width="189" height="190" srcset="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/ApplesOrangesrts_logo_round_blue-298x300.png 298w, https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/ApplesOrangesrts_logo_round_blue-150x150.png 150w, https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/ApplesOrangesrts_logo_round_blue-768x773.png 768w, https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/ApplesOrangesrts_logo_round_blue.png 1000w" sizes="(max-width: 189px) 100vw, 189px" /></p><p> </p><p> </p><p> </p><p> </p><p> </p><h1>Informatics 295/190: AR/VR Theater</h1><p>Professor Theresa Jean Tanenbaum, UC Irvine, Department of Informatics<br />Tim Kashani, Apples &amp; Oranges Productions<br />Zach Anderson, Apples &amp; Oranges Productions<br />Time: Mondays, 3-6pm<br />Location: Calit2 2100 (The Evoke Lab)</p><h1>Course Description</h1><p>This two quarter course will develop three experimental theater projects using augmented and virtual reality technology. These projects are being developed in collaboration with ICS Alum, and Broadway/Film producer and director Tim Kashani of Apples &amp; Oranges Arts.  Teams of student designers and developers from the Donald Bren School of Information &amp; Computer Sciences, and from the Claire Trevor School of the Arts will collaborate with professional artists, producers, and mentors to develop three different pieces of intellectual property that Tim has originated. The projects range from a current Broadway show to immersive new endeavors in storytelling. Similar to a capstone project class with a client, students will be on interdisciplinary teams, and will work closely with our clients and partners to design, develop and launch engaging new technologies for theater and performance that incorporate emerging technologies for AR and VR. This course incorporates a blend of traditional and self-motivated experiential learning using the same methodology applied in successful startups. Participation in this class is by application only.  Participating students have committed to both Winter and Spring quarters. We are looking for a diverse and creative interdisciplinary team that bridges specializations in ICS and the arts. This is an advanced class for upper division undergraduates and graduate students: maturity, responsibility, communication skills, professionalism, and work ethic are essential for success in this course.</p><p>IMPORTANT: This is a two quarter class and requires that students commit to both the Winter and Spring quarters! We will be working with intellectual property owned or licensed by our external collaborators, and so all course participants will be required to sign intellectual property agreements as a condition of their participation.</p><p> </p><h1>Course Logistics</h1><h2>Course Structure</h2><p>We meet once a week for three hours. These meetings are meant to be time for us to prototype ideas, share resources, workshop new ideas, and take risks. Our expectation is that the bulk of the work will be occurring outside the classroom, and that class time will be essentially a VR/AR research development laboratory for us to experiment and play.</p><h2>Course Website</h2><p>The syllabus will be posted on the course website, along with any digital resources for your use. This syllabus is a living document and we will update it throughout the class to reflect the evolution of our goals and deliverables.</p><h2>GitHub Repositories</h2><p>We will be using GitHub to store and track the assets created for the projects in this class. I will create private repositories for each team as part of the course setup. Every student should create a GitHub account if they do not already have one.</p><h2>Slack</h2><p>We will maintain a Slack Channel as part of the Transformative Play Lab workspace. I will distribute sign-up links in the first week.</p><h1>Grading and Evaluations</h1><h2>Grading Philosophy</h2><p>Grading in this class will be a bit untraditional. We have undergraduates and graduate students in this class, and grading culture is very different for these two groups (in grad school, a B- is considered a failing grade). Our projects are also driven by external factors and we are (or should be) intrinsically motivated for them to succeed on their own merits, and not because a grade is at stake.</p><p>While there will be milestones throughout the class, we will not have any explicit numeric grades, nor will there be any tests or exams.  I will be tracking attendance, using notecards (see below), as is my tradition.</p><h2>Course Contracts</h2><p>Rather than doing purely numeric grading, I will be running this class using a course contract model, similar to how my undergraduate experience worked. This will be new for most of you, but will be worthwhile. How do course contracts work?  There is a process:</p><ul><li><strong>Week 1: </strong>Each of you will write out several paragraphs discussing your expectations of the class, and of yourself over the quarter. What do you hope to learn? What do you hope to accomplish? How would you like to be evaluated? What do you expect from the instructional team? Please be as detailed and specific as possible.  This is an exercise in goal setting, and in scoping work that each of you will be doing. Because the class is so interdisciplinary and diverse, your contracts will vary substantially from each other.</li><li><strong>Week 2:</strong> I (Josh) will read your contracts and provide you with my comments and feedback. We will discuss what I think are realistic goals vs. unrealistic ones. I may ask you to push for more if I don’t think you’re being ambitious enough, or I might ask you to scale back your expectations a bit.  We will negotiate this, over e-mail, and at the end of the process we will produce a contract for your work in the class that we will both sign.</li><li><strong>Ongoing:</strong> I will regularly check in with you about the state of the projects and your own individual progress in the class. These will be informal, but will give us chances to course correct as needed, and revisit the contract periodically. If there is a major change in either of our expectations, we’ll add an addendum to the contract to reflect this.</li></ul><p>All students will receive “In Progress” as their grade at the end of the Winter Quarter. We will have a “mid-term” check-in to make sure we’re all still on the same page.  Moving into Spring, we will continue these check-ins.</p><h2>Narrative Evaluations</h2><p>At the end of the course we will do written evaluations:</p><ul><li><strong>Student Self Evaluation: </strong>At the end of the course you will revisit your contract and your accomplishments and write a several paragraph self-evaluation of your performance in the class. Did you learn what you set out learn? Did you succeed in your goals? What did you learn that you hadn’t anticipated learning? What lessons will you take with you from this class? What would you have done differently?</li><li><strong>Student Course Evaluation: </strong>You will also write an evaluation of the course as a whole. What succeeded from your point of view.  What failed? What could be done differently in the future?</li><li><strong>Faculty Evaluation of Student: </strong>I will read and review these evaluations, and I will write my own assessment of your performance, which will consider how well I believe you accomplished your goals in the class, and where I saw you struggling. For the purposes of your transcript, I will translate this qualitative assessment of your performance into the appropriate letter grade.</li></ul><h2>Attendance and Notecards</h2><p>Class attendance will be determined by completing index cards. The index cards are also a means for me to get feedback about the course. I will distribute the cards at the beginning of the class and collect them at the end. For each class please write your name on a card, the date, your student ID and a comment about the course. If you would like to submit an anonymous comment, take an extra card and don’t put your name on it.</p><h1>Course Related Outside Activities</h1><h2><a href="http://globalgamejam.org/">Global Game Jam</a></h2><p><img class="aligncenter" src="http://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/wp-content/transformativeplay/uploads/ggj17_website_presskitheader-300x169.jpg" width="424" height="239" /></p><p>Save the date now!  January 26-28, 2018! <a href="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/global-game-jam-2018/">I will be coordinating UCI’s GGJ location this year for the fourth year in a row</a>.  Previous GGJs have had amazing turnouts, with participants from UCI, other colleges in the area, and local game companies. This is a chance for you to polish your game design and development skills in an open and collaborative environment.  It will also be a chance to get to really get a sense of what your teams are capable of.  While participation in the Global Game Jam is not mandatory, it is <em>strongly </em>encouraged.</p><h2>Film Screenings</h2><p><img class="aligncenter" src="https://lunerontheatre.files.wordpress.com/2015/03/kingkong11.jpg" alt="Image result for king Kong the musical" width="524" height="340" /></p><p>We will be hosting several screenings of key media related to the class throughout the year, including showings of King Kong, and an AR/VR micro film festival.  These will be scheduled on an ad-hoc basis throughout the quarter, and attendance is optional.</p><h2>Star Wars Secrets of the Empire “Hyper Reality” Experience (optional – limited spots)</h2><p><img class="aligncenter" src="http://starwarsblog.starwars.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/08/star-wars-void-secrets-of-the-empire.jpg" alt="Image result for star wars secrets of the empire" width="515" height="224" /></p><p>We’re looking into doing a field trip to experience the new <a href="https://disneyland.disney.go.com/attractions/downtown-disney-district/star-wars-secrets-empire/">Star Wars VR experience</a> at Downtown Disney in Anaheim.  We will have limited spots available for this trip, and are still determining if it is feasible, but stay tuned for details.</p><h1>Code of Conduct and Course Culture</h1><p>We are a diverse and interdisciplinary group, and we may not always agree with each other in this classroom. My expectation is that everyone will act in a constructive, inclusive, and respectful manner in order to produce a collaborative environment where everyone is empowered to contribute fully. Central to any effective design practice is the ability to give and receive critical feedback in a manner that supports the goals of the project, builds up the abilities of your colleagues, and generates new creative opportunities. Certain behaviors undermine this process, and we will not permit them within the classroom.  We expect all participants in the class to abide by these guidelines for maintaining a constructive and inclusive environment*</p><p>* <em>many thanks to Bonnie &#8220;Bo&#8221; Ruberg for advice and language around inclusive behavior and conduct. These guidelines are adapted from their ICS 60 syllabus.</em></p><ul><li style="font-weight: 400;"><span style="font-weight: 400;"><strong>Refrain from using derogatory language</strong>, such as words with negative connotations that refer to a specific racial or ethnic group, or that are related to gender or sexual identity. Also avoid “ableist” language, words that use references to mental or physical disabilities as slurs.</span></li><li style="font-weight: 400;"><span style="font-weight: 400;"><strong>Check your privilege.</strong> Think about who in the room has had a chance to speak and make space for others to share their ideas. Instead of making blanket statements about what certain people or cultures are like, speak from the “I” &#8212; that is, draw from your own experience.</span></li><li style="font-weight: 400;"><span style="font-weight: 400;">Always use the <strong>correct gender pronouns and names</strong> for your classmates. Gender pronouns are words like she/her, he/him, or they/them. (If you would like the instructors to refer to you using a name other than the one listed on the course roster or you would like to notify us of your gender pronouns, please let us know.)</span></li></ul><p>At certain points throughout the course we will be doing more formal Critiques of the projects. For these critiques we will use a form of the <a href="https://lizlerman.com/critical-response-process/">Liz Lerhman Critical Response</a> process. The version we are employing here is one that has been used to great effect within UCI&#8217;s Sound Design program, and all credit for it lies with Professor Vinnie Oliveri. The process is as follows:</p><blockquote><p style="padding-left: 30px;">&#8220;The responsibilities of the responders are twofold:</p><p style="padding-left: 90px;">1) not to bring their own agenda to work they are responding to and</p><p style="padding-left: 90px;">2) have a desire for the artist to do her/his best work.</p><p style="padding-left: 30px;">Responders are attempting to help the artist create her/his piece, not to create their own. It is important for responders, as hard as this may be, to not bring their own bias and expectations to the process.</p><p style="padding-left: 30px;">The responsibility of the artist is to be honest and open. The artist needs to be in a place where they can question their own work in a somewhat public environment. Also, it is the motivation and meaning of the creator that is the basis on which feedback is given, so the artist should be very clear about her/his intent.</p><p style="padding-left: 60px;"><strong>PROCESS STEPS</strong></p><p style="padding-left: 60px;"><strong>1. Self-Statement: Goals and Assessment</strong><br />Artist identifies goals of the work and assesses those goals. Emphasize clarity in these statements. Consider how the goals may have changed as the work has changed. This helps the artist clarify the intent of the work and will help responders frame their responses.</p><p style="padding-left: 60px;"><strong>2. Affirmation and Observation</strong><br />Responders give the artist either positive feedback about the work or moments that affected them. People want to hear that what they have just completed has meaning. The artist must work to really hear the comments.  Responders need to try to make the palette of responses as wide as possible. Be specific and expansive in the use of vocabulary about the work.</p><p style="padding-left: 60px;"><b><strong>3.  Artist Questions Responders</strong></b><br />Artist has the time to ask the viewers questions about the work. Be specific; nothing is too insignificant. The more the artist clarifies what s/he is working on, the more meaningful becomes the dialogue.</p><p style="padding-left: 60px;"><b><strong>4.  Responders Question Artist</strong></b><br />Responders ask neutral questions of the artist about the work. It is very important not to be judgmental in the phrasing of the questions. This is a chance for the responders to help the artist step back and analyze the work. If given the chance, most criticisms can be stated or explored in this step in a neutral fashion.</p><p style="padding-left: 60px;"><strong>5.  Criticisms and Opinions</strong><br />If there is a criticism that can&#8217;t be stated in the form of a neutral question, responders can express opinions about the work to the artist after they have asked permission of the artist. The artist is allowed to refuse at any time. The opinions should be positive criticism, based on problem-solving techniques. It may seem redundant to ask permission for every single criticism, but it is very important. This gives the artist control of this very sensitive step and creates a dialogue, albeit a very basic one.</p></blockquote><h1>Milestones and Deliverables</h1><p>Our goal in this course will be to iterate rapidly through design ideas, to fail fast, and to learn through doing. Ultimately we will be producing experimental software that exists at the intersection of games, virtual reality, augmented reality, and theatre. We will be figuring out what that looks like together, and will be establishing appropriate milestones and deliverables as we go.  Right now this section is intentionally undefined, but it will become more specific as the projects take shape.</p><h2>Weekly Standups</h2><p>We will devote time each week for the project teams to present their current work.  This might be the results of research into the specific design and content domains that we’re working with, it might be a new prototype, or a design revision, it might be an idea or exercise to workshop with the class.</p><h2>Documentation</h2><p>Each team will be responsible for maintaining documentation of their projects, including design documents, requirements documents, timelines and task assignments, concept documents, and other suitable materials.</p><h1>Spring Schedule and Materials</h1><p>For the Spring quarter we will be moving out of our exploration phase and into a more formal development process. We will be using &#8220;Agile&#8221; development methods to split the quarter into 5 two-week long &#8220;sprints&#8221;. This means that things are going to get more demanding moving forward. Every two weeks, each team should have a prototype ready to review. While we will have some time in class to do standups and discuss project blockers, our expectation is that you will be meeting and working on these projects daily outside of class.</p><p><img src="https://cdn-8a82.kxcdn.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/02/scrum_process_afa_5000.jpg" alt="Image result for agile and scrum" /></p><h2>Agile Development Resources and SCRUM</h2><ul><li><a href="http://agilemanifesto.org/">The Agile Manifesto</a></li><li><a href="https://www.agilealliance.org/agile101/agile-glossary/">A glossary of common Agile terminology</a></li><li><a href="http://scrummethodology.com/">Overview of Scrum</a></li><li><a href="http://www.scrumguides.org/">Scrum Guides</a></li></ul><h2>Weekly Schedule</h2><h3>Week 1: <strong>April 2nd</strong></h3><ul><li>Welcome new members</li><li>Discuss recruiting needs</li><li>Discuss infrastructure needs</li><li>Overview Audience Interactivity Workshop</li><li>Team Standups/Meetings</li><li>For next week:<ul><li>Read: Striner et al. &#8211; A Common Framework for Audience Interactivity (to be distributed on Slack)</li></ul></li></ul><h3>Week 2: <strong>April 9th</strong></h3><ul><li>Audience Interactivity Workshop (With special guest, Alina Striner)</li></ul><h3>Week 3: <strong>April 16th</strong></h3><ul><li>First Sprint Reviews</li></ul><h3>Week 4: <strong>April 23th</strong></h3><ul><li>Professor Tanenbaum @ CHI</li><li>Open Lab Time</li></ul><h3>Week 5: <strong>April 30th</strong></h3><ul><li>Second Sprint Reviews</li></ul><h3>Week 6: <strong>May 7th</strong></h3><ul><li>TBD</li></ul><h3>Week 7: <strong>May 14th</strong></h3><ul><li>Third Sprint Reviews</li></ul><h3>Week 8: <strong>May 21st</strong></h3><ul><li>Logistics for final showcase</li><li>TBD</li></ul><h3>Week 9: <strong>May 28th</strong></h3><ul><li>Fourth Sprint Reviews</li></ul><h3>Week 10: <strong>June 4th</strong></h3><ul><li>Showcase prep</li><li>TBD</li></ul><h3>Finals Week: <strong>June 11th or date TBD?</strong></h3><ul><li>Final Review/Showcase</li></ul><h1>Winter Schedule and Materials</h1><p>We will meet weekly to discuss new content, present current state of our projects, workshop new ideas, and play relevant games. There are readings each week that are mandatory, as well as some recommended additional readings that are optional. This schedule will change and evolve, in particular as project milestones come online, so be prepared for more work and deliverables to be added as we narrow in on specific project objectives.</p><h3>Optional Reading List (Books)</h3><ul><li><a href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/B011I5QMRA/ref=dp-kindle-redirect?_encoding=UTF8&amp;btkr=1">Jack Viertel &#8211; The Secret Life of the American Musical: How Broadway Shows are Built</a></li><li><a 3D Works and How to Use It</a></li><li><a href="https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/1970001127/ref=oh_aui_search_detailpage?ie=UTF8&amp;psc=1">Jason Jerald &#8211; The VR Book: Human-Centered Design for style="color: #ff0000;">Deliverables Due: </span>By the <strong><em><u>start</u></em></strong> of week 3’s class, you should have:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">A first draft of your deliverables</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Get specific about what you hope to achieve by Week 10 (we’ll look back at this during your final presentation for this quarter)</span></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li><li>Activity: Ninja Slap!</li><li>Free VR/AR Play Time</li><li>For next week:<ul><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1bW6HZgXFeTUN0EQHNAJ-Gi45Kl9eZxtt/view?usp=sharing">Read Sanford Meisner on Acting</a></li><li>Individually:<a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1cpczHkM5Aq3i4cy3uBiNzirZV3GtZ3BI/view?usp=sharing"> Read Brant Pope – Redefining Acting: The Implications of the Meisner Method</a></li></ul></li></ul><h2>Week 5 – February 5<sup>th</sup></h2><ul><li><a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1IO1j_WSH2kkYuOp8qJm8v6OgWRz3hS8s/view?usp=sharing">Micro-lecture: The Meisner Method</a></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Deliverables: By the <strong><em><u>start</u></em></strong> of class, you should have:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Your Concept Document</span></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">A prepared pitch presentation that you’ll present to the class today</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">The presentation should have a progress update and we should see what you’ve done so far</span></li></ul></li></ul></li><li>Activity: Repetition Exercises?</li><li>For next week:<ul><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1FkfJk140hFrndMJsOMwOii5KSQX6RII5/view?usp=sharing">Read David Saltz – The Reality of Doing: Real Speech Acts in the Theatre</a></li><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1TefBAshRaSpwCGHPMn7HcJ8DmdGxYK6d/view?usp=sharing">Read Tanenbaum &amp; Tanenbaum: Commitment to Meaning: A Reframing of Agency in Games</a></li></ul></li></ul><h2>Week 6 – February 12<sup>th</sup></h2><ul><li><a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1-tARSn4nw8zv76Zn5UgJs4teJjDaqWss/view?usp=sharing"><del>Micro </del>MACRO!-lecture: Agency as Commitment to Meaning</a></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Deliverables: By the <strong><em><u>start</u></em></strong> of class, you should have:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">First draft of your VR Design Document</span></li></ul></li><li>Activity: TBD</li><li>For next week:<ul><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1d4Ja6FovMKgbNDP_aZkabiVDYvkpw7ue/view?usp=sharing">Read Jeff Wirth – Interactive Acting (selections)</a></li><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1xXyPOxIY9sg81YvvdVe7C5N5a6Pf2a8W/view?usp=sharing">Read Tanenbaum – Dissertation Introduction &amp; Conclusion</a></li><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1vZK4PMH41WENT8LQyS9ITz_PPtmjy7fn/view?usp=sharing">Read Bizzocchi et al. – Games, Narrative, and the Design of Interface</a></li><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/10u5wwCkK4IutgPbU6zkaHouCi26DxvnK/view?usp=sharing">Read Tanenbaum &amp; Bizzocchi – Rock Band: A Case Study in the Design of Embodied Interface Experience</a></li></ul></li></ul><h2>Week 7 – February 19<sup>th</sup></h2><p>(Note: This Monday is President&#8217;s Day. There is no formal class, but the lab will be open and we will have free VR/AR Play Time gear set up)</p><ul><li>Open lab</li><li>Free VR/AR Play Time</li></ul><h2>Week 8 – February 26<sup>th</sup></h2><ul><li>Micro-lecture: Embodied and Narrativized Interfaces</li><li>Activity: Cardboard Interface Prototyping with MaKey MaKey</li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Deliverables:<em>(we will tweak and add/remove things to this as we get closer to this week) </em>By the <strong><em><u>start</u></em></strong> of class, you should have:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Updated VR Design Document</span></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Status update/check-in</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">This will be in the form of an updated Pitch presentation (this will be our check-in on pitch progress before we get to week 10)</span></li></ul></li></ul></li><li>For next week:<ul><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1JWcir0lrCQxjm8jQITp6dGgFiKw8O9o9/view?usp=sharing">Read Tanenbaum et al. – Costumes and Wearables as Game Controllers</a></li><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1mqLxlA7CBFANCWzjYpoAahXNjn1CeCal/view?usp=sharing">Read Tanenbaum &amp; Tanenbaum – Envisioning the Future of Wearable Play: Conceptual Models for Props and Costumes as Game Controllers</a></li></ul></li></ul><h2>Week 9 – March 5<sup>th</sup></h2><ul><li>Workshop: Costumes as Game Controllers</li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Deliverables:<em>(we will tweak and add/remove things to this as we get closer to this week) </em>By the <strong><em><u>start</u></em></strong> of class, you should have:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Status Update</span></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Necessary Resources for Success in Q2 First Draft</span></li></ul></li><li>For next week:<ul><li>Individually: <a href="https://drive.google.com/file/d/1pqe4p8kY5YvdnnuHVJ2-uPGoQ79_A-Pw/view?usp=sharing">Read Tanenbaum &amp; Tanenbaum – Empathy and Identity in Digital Games: Towards a New Theory of Transformative Play</a></li></ul></li></ul><h2>Week 10 – March 12<sup>th</sup></h2><ul><li>Micro-Lecture: Transformative Play and VR</li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Deliverables: By the <em><u>start</u></em> of class, you should have:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Final draft of Necessary Resources for Success in Q2</span></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Final Pitch Presentation:</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Elevator Pitch</span></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Details around project (including a detailed status update)</span></li><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Timeline and Deliverables for Q2</span><ul><li><span style="color: #ff0000;">Did we meet what we said we would in Week 5?</span></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul><h2>Finals Week – March 19<sup>th</sup></h2><ul><li>Mid-point check-ins</li></ul><h1>Additional Important Course Policies and Resources</h1><h2>Academic Honesty</h2><p>Please read and heed the following information regarding academic dishonesty.<em> <strong>The instructor cannot and will not tolerate academic dishonesty.</strong> </em>For more information, refer to the UCI Student Handbook. The UCI campus policy on academic honesty resides here: <a href="http://honesty.uci.edu/">http://honesty.uci.edu/</a></p><p>The penalty for plagiarism is at a minimum to receive a 0 on the assignment and have the case reported to the Associate Dean’s office. Particularly flagrant cases may receive more severe punishment (notably failing the course).</p><h3><strong><em>What is cheating?</em></strong></h3><ul><li><em>Supplying or using work or answers that are not your own.</em></li><li><em>Providing or accepting assistance with completing assignments or examinations.</em></li><li><em>Faking data or results.</em></li><li><em>Interfering in any way with someone else’s work.</em></li><li><em>Stealing an examination or solution from the teacher.</em></li></ul><h3><strong><em>What is plagiarism?</em></strong></h3><ul><li><em>Copying a paper from a source text without proper acknowledgment.</em></li><li><em>Buying a paper from a research service or term paper mill.</em></li><li><em>Turning in another student’s work with or without that student’s knowledge. </em></li><li><em>Copying a paper from a source text without proper acknowledgment.</em></li><li><em>Copying materials from a source text, supplying proper documentation, but leaving out quotation marks.</em></li><li><em>Paraphrasing materials from a source text without appropriate documentation.</em></li><li><em>Turning in a paper from a term paper website.</em></li></ul><p>You should be on guard against plagiarism at all times.  At any time that you read anything in preparation for a paper or consciously recall anything that you have read or heard, you must be prepared to provide documentation.</p><p>Generally, when you use someone else’s ideas and/or words, you will either quote that person directly or you will paraphrase or summarize that person’s words. You must let the reader know which you are doing.</p><ol><li>If you quote the source directly, you must<ol><li>Put quotation marks before and after that person’s words</li><li>Let the reader know the source by (1) putting a footnote or endnote number at the end of the quotation, or (2) putting at least the source’s name in parentheses after the quotation marks (such as when being taken from fieldwork).</li></ol></li><li>If you paraphrase (a paraphrase is about the same length as the original, but in different words) or if you summarize (a summary is a severely shortened version of the original), you must<ol><li>Introduce the source in some manner at the beginning of the passage being paraphrased (or summarized) so that the reader can tell where your idea stops and the other person’s begins</li><li>State the ideas taken from the source in your own words and your own arrangement. It is possible to plagiarize sentence patterns as well as exact words. A handy rule: if, in a paraphrase or summary, you use a stretch of more than three words in their exact order from a source, you should put those words into quotation marks</li><li>Provide an exact source citation for those ideas paraphrased or summarized. This may be done either by footnote/endnote number at the end of the passages or by parenthetical references to the work and page(s). This citation provides credit to the author being used and allows the reader access to the material for further study.</li></ol></li><li>You must also provide a footnote for any chart, graph, figure, table, summary, or other data taken directly from another source or any information derived from such materials. You should also be sure to check copyright as to whether you are allowed to use this figure.</li></ol><p>For example, the text here on plagiarism was initially written Gillian Hayes for the Winter 2013 version of INF 242, although some modifications and additions of my own have been integrated into it.  The original can be found here:<em> </em><a href="http://www.gillianhayes.com/Inf242w13/"><em>http://www.gillianhayes.com/Inf242w13/</em></a>, along with Professor Hayes’ own disclaimer that the material has been “generously borrowed and slightly modified from the UTC Center for Advisement and Student Success.”</p><h2>Special Accommodations</h2><p>If you are a student with a disability (<em>e.g.</em>, physical, learning, psychiatric, vision, hearing, etc.) and think that you might need special assistance or a special accommodation in this class or any other class, please check out the Disability Center <a href="http://www.disability.uci.edu/">online</a> or visit them in person at: 100 Disability Services Center, Building 313, Irvine, CA 92697-5130.   If you are having difficulty with the class for any of these reasons, please let me know so that I can work with you to meet your learning needs. If for any reason you are uncomfortable discussing the details surrounding a given situation you need not disclose anything, but at least let me know that something is going on so that arrangements can be made to adjust things for you <em>before</em> you fall too far behind.</p><p>I am always available to meet with students who are having trouble, and I am usually willing to make some reasonable accommodations if you have a legitimate issue, but I <strong>require</strong> that you check-in with me <strong>before</strong> a problem gets out of control so we can work something out. If you come to me during finals week to explain that you were in the hospital for 6 weeks and couldn’t complete your work, my first question will be: “why am I just now finding this out?”</p><h2>Counseling Center</h2><p>Likewise, if you find that personal problems, career indecision, study and time management difficulties, etc. are adversely impacting your successful progress at UCI, please check out the Counseling Center or Student Services. College can often have adverse effects on one’s physical and mental health, and it is better to seek help early than allow the trials of pursing and advanced degree to cause serious harm.</p><p> </p><p> </p></div>

(2012). <a href="http://bst.sagepub.com/content/32/5/393.short">Mass Effect 2: A Case Study in the Design of Game Narrative.</a> Bulletin of Science Technology Society October 2012 vol. 32 no. 5 393-404</p><p>Bizzocchi, J., Lin, B., Tanenbaum, T. (2011). <a href="http://www.inderscience.com/info/inarticle.php?artid=43445">Games, Narrative, and the Design of Interface.</a> <a href="http://www.inderscience.com/browse/index.php?journalCODE=ijart">The International Journal of Art and Technology (IJART)</a> 4(4) 460-479</p><p>Tanenbaum, T. 				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
			<h2 class="elementor-heading-title elementor-size-default"><b><span style="font-size:24.0pt;line-height:107%;
font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;,serif;mso-fareast-font-family:&quot;Times New Roman&quot;;
mso-font-kerning:18.0pt;mso-ansi-language:EN-US;mso-fareast-language:EN-US;
mso-bidi-language:AR-SA">Transformative Costumed Play &amp; Narrative Interfaces</span></b></h2>		</div>
				</div>
						</div>
			</div>
		</div>
						</div>
			</div>
		</section>
				<section class="elementor-element elementor-element-e34ef35 elementor-section-boxed elementor-section-height-default elementor-section-height-default elementor-section elementor-top-section" data-id="e34ef35" data-element_type="section" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
						<div class="elementor-container elementor-column-gap-default">
				<div class="elementor-row">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-2ed531a elementor-column elementor-col-100 elementor-top-column" data-id="2ed531a" data-element_type="column">
			<div class="elementor-column-wrap  elementor-element-populated">
					<div class="elementor-widget-wrap">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-6897c6d elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="6897c6d" data-element_type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
				<div class="elementor-widget-container">
					<div class="elementor-text-editor elementor-clearfix"><p><strong>Drawing on practices from theater and the performing arts we are creating games that use costumes to help elicit a sense of character identification and identity transformation from players, and studying the impact that wearing costumes and masks has on socially co-located play and solo play. We look at how the body of the player can be treated as a form of &#8220;narrativized interface&#8221; through the design of embodied interactions.</strong></p><h3><span style="color: #00ccff;"><strong>Associated Projects</strong></span></h3><ul><li><a href="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/magia-transformo/">Magia Transformo &#8211; The Dance of Transformation</a></li><li><a href="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/research/costumes-and-wearables-as-game-controllers/">Costumes as Game Controllers Studio at TEI</a></li><li><a href="https://transformativeplay.ics.uci.edu/costumes-wearables-as-game-controllers-workshop-at-uci/">Costumes as Game Controllers Workshop at UCI</a></li><li>Costumed Gaming Study</li><li>Theatrical Play Book</li></ul><h3><strong><span style="color: #00ccff;">Associated Publications</span></strong></h3><ul><li><a href="https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/978-3-540-89454-4.pdf#page=262">2008 ICIDS Paper: Improvisation and Performance as Models for Interacting with Stories</a></li><li><a href="http://dl.acm.org/citation.cfm?id=1581093">2009 SIGGRAPH Paper: Rock Band: A Case Study in the Design of Embodied Interface Experience</a></li><li><a href="http://www.inderscienceonline.com/doi/abs/10.1504/IJART.2011.043445">2011 International Journal of Art + Technology Article: Games Narrative and the Design of Interface</a></li><li><a href="https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/978-3-642-25289-1.pdf#page=68">2011 ICIDS Paper: Being in the Story: Readerly Pleasure, Acting Theory, and Performing a Role</a></li><li><a href="http://dl.acm.org/citation.cfm?id=2481205">2013 C&amp;C Paper: The Effects of Physicality on the Child&#8217;s Imagination</a></li><li><a href="https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-02756-2_18">2013 ICIDS Paper: <em>Performative Authoring: </em> Nurturing Storytelling in Children through Imaginative Enactment</a></li><li><a href="http://summit.sfu.ca/item/15285">2015 PhD Dissertation: Identity Transformation and Agency in Digital Narratives and Story Based Games</a></li><li><a href="http://dl.acm.org/citation.cfm?id=2683584">2015 TEI Studio: Costumes and Wearables as Game Controllers</a></li><li><a href="https://pdfs.semanticscholar.org/9fd4/7248449651cb9cb747ececa93caa988c40f2.pdf">2015 FDG Paper: Envisioning the Future of Wearable Play: Conceptual Models for Props and Costumes as Game Controllers</a></li><li><a href="https://pdfs.semanticscholar.org/9810/81c2cf4eaf7a70bd7c864646594082cd3d3c.pdf?_ga=2.253556875.361291960.1501917737-212206059.1501917737">2015 FDG Paper: Empathy and Identity in Digital Games: Towards a New Theory of Transformative Play </a></li><li><a href="http://www.qgcon.com/2015-speakers/cosplayed-gaming-exploring-gender-identity-through-costumed-play/">2015 QGCon Talk: Cosplayed Gaming: Exploring Gender Identity through Costumed Play</a></li><li><a href="http://www.digra.org/digital-library/publications/itsa-me-mario-costumed-gamings-effects-on-character-identification/">2016 DiGRA Talk: “ITS’A ME, MARIO!”: COSTUMED GAMING’S EFFECTS ON CHARACTER IDENTIFICATION</a></li><li><a href="http://analoggamestudies.org/2017/07/2716/">2017 Analog Game Studies Article: Chalk, Props, and Costumes: Two Exercises for Teaching Pervasive Game Design</a></li></ul></div>
				</div>

                            <div class="section-title page-title">
								<h1 class="main-title">INF 190 Games: From Concept to Pitchable Prototype</h1>                                <div class="bread-crumb" typeof="BreadcrumbList" vocab="http://schema.org/">
									                                </div>
                            </div>

    <div class="content-inner">
